2:26:1294:28:28:273:338:616:12:Times:12:Times:A:ABSTRACT
2:27:1302:28:14:72:540:588:12:g_d0_f1:12:g_d0_f1:F:We propose a theory that rising globalization and rising wage inequality are related because trade liberalization
2:28:1415:14:14:72:540:574:12:g_d0_f1:12:g_d0_f1:F:raises the demand facing highly competitive skill-intensive firms. In our model, only the lowest-cost
2:29:1516:14:14:72:540:560:12:g_d0_f1:12:g_d0_f1:F:firms participate in the global economy exactly along the lines of Melitz (2003). In addition to differing
2:30:1622:14:14:72:540:546:12:g_d0_f1:12:g_d0_f1:F:in their productivity, firms differ in their skill intensity. We model skill-biased technology as a correlation
2:31:1733:14:14:72:540:532:12:g_d0_f1:12:g_d0_f1:F:between skill intensity and technological acumen, and we estimate this correlation to be large using
2:32:1833:14:14:72:540:518:12:g_d0_f1:12:g_d0_f1:F:firm-level data from Chile in 1995. A fall in trade costs leads to both greater trade volumes and an
2:33:1933:14:14:72:540:504:12:g_d0_f1:12:g_d0_f1:F:increase in the relative demand for skill, as the lowest-cost/most-skilled firms expand to serve the
2:34:2033:14:14:72:540:490:12:g_d0_f1:12:g_d0_f1:F:export market while less skill-intensive non-exporters retrench in the face of increased import competition.
2:35:2141:14:14:72:540:476:12:g_d0_f1:12:g_d0_f1:F:This mechanism works regardless of factor endowment differences, so we provide an explanation
2:36:2234:14:14:72:540:462:12:g_d0_f1:12:g_d0_f1:F:for why globalization and wage inequality move together in both skill-abundant and skill-scarce countries.
2:37:2340:14:14:72:540:448:12:g_d0_f1:12:g_d0_f1:F:In our model countries are net exporters of the services of their abundant factor, but there are no Stolper-
2:38:2448:14:42:72:442:434:12:g_d0_f1:12:g_d0_f1:C:Samuelson effects because import competition affects all domestic firms equally.

3:74:4343:27:22:256:355:323:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:A:1.  INTRODUCTION
3:75:4359:22:12:86:539:301:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:A:Two of the most striking trends in the global economy since 1970 are globalization and increasing wage
3:76:4461:12:12:71:539:289:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:B:inequality.  For example, in the United States, the premium that college graduates earn over high school
3:77:4565:12:12:71:539:277:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:B:graduates grew by 35 percentage points between 1971 and 2005 (Autor, Katz, and Kearney (2008)).  Over
3:78:4666:12:12:71:539:265:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:B:the same period, the ratio of trade to GDP in the U.S. grew 15 percentage points.2Similar trends are
3:79:4766:12:12:72:540:253:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:B:apparent around the world, including in many developing countries (Goldberg and Pavcnik (2007)).  This
3:80:4868:12:12:72:540:241:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:B:raises an important but di¢ cult question for applied economics: has increased globalization contributed to
3:81:4975:12:12:72:540:229:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:B:growing wage inequality?  More precisely, have reductions in the costs of cross-border transactions led to
3:82:5081:12:12:72:323:217:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:B:both greater globalization and increased wage inequality?
3:83:5138:12:12:86:539:205:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:A:There is a large, fascinating, and inconclusive literature on this question.  The primary alternative
3:84:5239:12:12:72:540:193:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:B:hypothesis is technological: skill-biased technological change, especially when embodied in information and
3:85:5346:12:12:72:540:181:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:B:communications technology investment, has led to an increased relative demand for more educated workers
3:86:5449:12:12:72:540:169:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:B:(see, for example, Autor, Levy, and Murnane (2003)).  In this view, globalization is a sideshow, having only
3:87:5557:12:12:72:356:157:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:B:a small e§ect on the skill premium, at least in the United States.
3:88:5623:12:12:86:539:145:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:A:In this paper we revisit this question using a novel approach.  In our model, Örms are heterogeneous
3:89:5723:12:14:72:540:133:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:B:in their productivity, and only the lowest-cost Örms participate in the global economy, exactly along the
3:90:5828:14:13:83:540:119:6:g_d0_f2:8:g_d0_f2:F:1Department  of  Economics,   University  of  Virginia,   Charlottesville,   VA  22904,   james.harrigan@virginia.edu,

3:92:6067:9:9:72:540:97:8:g_d0_f2:8:g_d0_f2:F:John McLaren, Maxim Engers, Eric Young, Latchezar Popov, «aàglarΩOzden, and seminar audiences in North America and

3:94:6225:7:0:83:297:81:6:g_d0_f2:8:g_d0_f2:A:2Our calculations, from United States National Accounts.
4:95:6282:0:12:72:540:710:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:B:lines of Melitz (2003).   In addition to di§ering in their productivity, Örms in our model di§er in their
4:96:6387:12:12:72:540:698:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:B:skill intensity.  We model skill-biased technology as a correlation between skill intensity and technological
4:97:6496:12:12:72:540:686:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:B:acumen, a speciÖcation strongly supported by both data and theory.  There is a large body of work that
4:98:6598:12:12:72:540:674:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:B:indicates that throughout the 20th century newer and more e¢ cient technologies have typically demanded
4:99:6701:12:12:72:540:662:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:B:more skilled (or better educated) workers; see Goldin and Katz (2008) and references therein. Acemoglu
4:100:6803:12:12:72:540:650:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:B:(2002) provides a theoretical framework to explain this phenomenon, as well as the acceleration in the bias in
4:101:6913:12:12:72:540:638:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:B:favor of skilled labor post 1979 in the U.S. New technologies are embodied in new goods, and Xiang (2005)
4:102:7018:12:12:72:540:626:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:B:shows that new goods have higher skill intensity.  Abowd, Haltiwanger, Lane, McKinney, and Sandusky
4:103:7117:12:12:72:540:614:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:B:(2007) Önd a strong positive correlation between advanced technology and skill (both measured in various
4:104:7221:12:12:72:281:602:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:B:ways) in a cross-sectional analysis of U.S. Örms.
4:105:7270:12:12:86:539:590:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:A:Skill-biased technological heterogeneity implies that, on average, the most competitive Örms are also the
4:106:7375:12:12:72:540:578:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:B:most skill-intensive.  As a consequence, a fall in trade costs leads to both greater trade volumes and an
4:107:7480:12:12:72:540:566:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:B:increase in the relative demand for skill, as the lowest-cost/most-skilled Örms expand to serve the export
4:108:7586:12:12:72:540:554:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:B:market while less skill-intensive non-exporters retrench in the face of increased import competition.  Thus,
4:109:7694:12:12:72:540:542:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:B:trade liberalization leads directly to both greater trade volumes and an increase in the demand for skill. Cru-
4:110:7805:12:12:72:540:530:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:B:cially, as long as productivity and skill intensity are positively correlated around the world, this mechanism
4:111:7915:12:12:72:540:518:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:B:works regardless of factor endowment di§erences. Thus, we provide an explanation for why globalization
4:112:8017:12:12:72:439:506:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:B:and wage inequality move together in both skill-abundant and skill-scarce countries.
4:113:8101:12:12:86:539:494:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:A:Some other models also predict that trade liberalization may increase the skill premium globally, including
4:114:8208:12:12:72:540:482:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:B:Feenstra and Hanson (1985), Acemoglu (2003), Zhu and Treáer (2005), and Burstein and Vogel (2010).
4:115:8306:12:12:72:540:470:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:B:What is new in our model is the interaction between skill intensity, factor endowment di§erences, and Örm
4:116:8411:12:11:72:540:458:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:B:heterogeneity.  This means that our model is consistent with the evidence on Örm-level heterogeneity and
4:117:8515:11:12:72:506:447:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:B:exporting (see Bernard, Jensen, Redding, and Schott (2007) for a lucid discussion of this evidence).
4:118:8615:12:12:86:539:435:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:A:In our numerical analysis, we calibrate the model to Örm-level data from a small open economy, Chile in
4:119:8718:12:12:72:540:423:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:B:1995. Using the calibrated model, we show how multilateral trade liberalization raises average productivity
4:120:8825:12:12:72:511:411:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:B:and real GDP, and also increases the skill premium in both skill-abundant and skill-scarce countries.
4:121:8926:12:12:86:539:399:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:A:Our paper builds on a large theoretical and empirical literature in international trade and labor economics.
4:122:9034:12:12:72:540:387:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:B:Two recent papers are most closely related to ours. Bernard, Redding, and Schott (2007) connect the Melitz
4:123:9140:12:12:72:540:375:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:B:model to the classic2-2-2Heckscher-Ohlin-Samuelson model, and thereby integrate factor endowment
4:124:9236:12:12:71:539:363:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:B:di§erences with Örm-level productivity and factor intensity di§erences. The model of Bernard, Redding, and
4:125:9342:12:12:71:539:351:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:B:Schott (2007) delivers a Stolper-Samuelson-like theorem, and as such does not predict that relative factor
4:126:9448:12:12:71:539:339:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:B:prices will move in the same direction in both trading countries. Burstein and Vogel (2010) work in a perfect
4:127:9557:12:12:71:539:327:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:B:competition framework that has no role for Örm heterogeneity, but their elegant treatment of skill-biased
4:128:9662:12:12:71:539:315:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:B:technology and its interaction with factor proportions o§ers an explanation for the rising skill premium in
4:129:9769:12:12:71:296:303:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:B:North and South that is similar to our explanation.
4:130:9820:12:12:86:539:291:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:A:Two other closely related papers are Yeaple (2005) and Vannoorenberghe (2011). These two models of
4:131:9918:12:12:71:539:279:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:B:Örm heterogeneity and trade feature skilled and unskilled workers, and Önd that trade liberalization raises
4:132:10025:12:12:71:539:267:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:B:the relative demand for skill and thus the skill premium. The mechanism in Yeaple (2005) operates purely
4:133:10129:12:12:71:539:255:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:B:within Örms, and thus rules out the empirically important between-Örm compositional e§ects that we study.
4:134:10234:12:12:71:539:243:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:B:Most importantly, both Yeaple (2005) and Vannoorenberghe (2011) analyze trade between identical countries
4:135:10339:12:12:71:539:231:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:B:only, and thus does not address the e§ects of factor endowment di§erences that are a key feature of our
4:136:10442:12:12:71:217:219:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:B:model and of the global economy.
4:137:10474:12:12:86:539:207:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:A:Models in the Heckscher-Ohlin tradition connect preferences to production technology, in the sense that
4:138:10577:12:11:71:539:195:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:B:the elasticity of substitution in demand is greater within goods of the same factor intensity. For example,
4:139:10684:11:12:71:540:184:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:B:in the canonical2-2-2model, goods with the same factor intensity are homogeneous and therefore have
4:140:10783:12:12:71:539:172:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:B:an inÖnite elasticity of substitution across "varieties". We depart from this tradition, instead treating goods
4:141:10894:12:12:71:539:160:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:B:symmetrically in demand, so that the elasticity of substitution in demand is independent of the skill intensity
4:142:11005:12:12:71:539:148:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:B:of goodsíproduction. This is an intuitive assumption (why should preferences and production technology
4:143:11107:12:12:71:540:136:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:B:be relateda priori?)  which has striking implications.  In particular, it implies that changes in import
4:144:11211:12:12:71:539:124:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:B:competition a§ect all import-competing Örms symmetrically.  This contrasts with the Stolper-Samuelson
4:145:11312:12:12:71:539:112:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:B:mechanism that is a feature of all Heckscher-Ohlin models, where the factor content of imports changes
4:146:11414:12:12:71:539:100:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:B:relative demands for import competing goods. In our model the connection between trade liberalization and
4:147:11519:12:12:71:330:88:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:B:factor prices operates through an entirely di§erent channel.
4:148:11579:12:0:86:539:76:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:A:Our model treats each Örmís production technology as Öxed, with the factor market e§ects of trade
5:149:11677:0:12:72:540:710:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:B:liberalization due to a composition e§ect: high-skill Örms gain market share globally at the expense of less
5:150:11785:12:12:72:540:698:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:B:skill-intensive Örms. A complementary mechanism, which is not incorporated in our model, is that highly
5:151:11888:12:12:72:540:686:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:B:productive Örms increase their skill intensity when faced with new export opportunities. This channel has
5:152:11993:12:12:72:540:674:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:B:been studied in a partial equilibrium framework by Bustos (2011), who Önds that Argentinian exporters
5:153:12094:12:12:72:540:662:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:B:invested in skill-upgrading in response to liberalized trade with Brazil, with liberalization leading to about
5:154:12204:12:12:72:540:650:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:B:a two percentage point increase in the skill share for big relative to small Örms.3Verhoogen (2008) Önds
5:155:12308:12:12:72:540:638:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:B:that peso devaluation raised within-plant wage inequality in Mexican manufacturing, and that this e§ect
5:156:12411:12:12:72:540:626:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:B:was stronger for initially more productive Örms. Verhoogen (2008) plausibly interprets this result as sup-
5:157:12517:12:12:72:540:614:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:B:port for within-plant quality and skill upgrading. A closely related general equilibrium theory of exporters
5:158:12625:12:12:72:540:602:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:B:endogenously adopting more skilled technologies is developed by Yeaple (2005). As noted above, however,
5:159:12728:12:12:72:540:590:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:B:Yeapleís model only considers trade between identical countries, and thus does not address the interactions
5:160:12835:12:12:72:429:578:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:B:between technology, trade and factor endowment di§erences that are our concern.
5:161:12914:12:12:86:539:566:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:A:Other empirical studies have failed to Önd large e§ects of trade liberalization on Örm-level or plant-level
5:162:13021:12:12:72:540:554:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:B:skill upgrading. In their ináuential early work, Bernard and Jensen (1997) and Bernard and Jensen (1999)
5:163:13125:12:12:72:540:542:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:B:Önd that the export-related skill-upgrading of U.S. manufacturing was predominantly due to employment
5:164:13226:12:12:72:540:530:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:B:shifts that favor skill-intensive plants, rather than di§erentially rapid skill-upgrading by exporters. Similarly,
5:165:13340:12:12:72:540:518:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:B:Treáer (2004) Önds that more skilled Canadian manufacturing plants expanded their relative employment
5:166:13441:12:12:72:540:506:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:B:shares after trade liberalization with the United States, but did not engage in skill upgrading. We show below
5:167:13551:12:12:72:540:494:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:B:that more skilled Chilean manufacturing plants are more likely to be exporters, but their skill intensity is not
5:168:13663:12:12:72:540:482:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:B:a§ected by the export decision. This empirical evidence for the United States, Canada, and Chile is consistent
5:169:13773:12:12:72:540:470:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:B:with the mechanism in our model. Incorporating the partial equilibrium theoretical insights of Bustos (2011)
5:170:13881:12:11:72:540:458:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:B:and Verhoogen (2008) into our general equilibrium framework would render our model intractable, so we
5:171:13982:11:12:72:540:447:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:B:focus exclusively in what follows on between-Örm rather than within-Örm e§ects of trade liberalization on
5:172:14087:12:12:72:540:435:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:B:relative skill demand. It is clear that within-Örm skill upgrading in response to trade liberalization will have
5:173:14199:12:27:72:472:423:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:B:e§ects on the equilibrium skill premium that are complementary to the channel we analyze.
5:174:14288:27:34:275:336:396:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:A:2.  THEORY
5:175:14298:34:12:86:539:362:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:A:In the Melitz model, there is one factor of production, and Örms are identical up to a Hicks neutral
5:176:14398:12:12:72:539:350:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:B:productivity parameter'that shifts marginal cost.  In an important paper, Bernard, Redding, and Schott
5:177:14500:12:12:72:540:338:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:B:(2007) combine the Melitz model with the classic2-2-2Heckscher-Ohlin-Samuelson model, which yields
5:178:14598:12:12:72:540:326:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:B:rich interactions between Örm heterogeneity and factor proportions di§erences across sectors and countries.
5:179:14705:12:12:72:540:314:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:B:Our model takes a di§erent approach to combining Örm heterogeneity with factor proportions di§erences: we
5:180:14810:12:12:72:540:302:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:B:assume that Örms di§er continuously intwodimensions, productivity and skill intensity. Just as Melitzías-
5:181:14915:12:12:72:540:290:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:B:sumption of heterogeneous Örm productivity was motivated by the evidence, our assumption of heterogeneity
5:182:15020:12:12:72:540:278:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:B:in skill intensity is motivated by the fact that skill intensity varies at least as much within conventionally-
5:183:15131:12:12:72:540:266:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:B:deÖned industries as it does between. Dunne, Foster, Haltiwanger, and Troske (2004) Önd this for the United
5:184:15238:12:12:72:429:254:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:B:States (see their Figure 1), and we Önd it in our Chilean Örm dataset (see below).
5:185:15320:12:12:86:539:242:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:A:In incorporating a continuum of skill intensities we depart from the assumption, common to the entire
5:186:15421:12:12:72:540:230:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:B:Heckscher-Ohlin tradition in trade theory, that the elasticity of substitution in demand is higher between
5:187:15527:12:12:72:540:218:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:B:varieties produced with a common factor intensity than it is between goods produced with di§erent factor
5:188:15631:12:11:72:540:206:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:B:intensities. Instead, we assume that the elasticity of substitution between all goods is the same. This implies
5:189:15742:11:12:72:532:195:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:B:that there are no Stolper-Samuelson e§ects because import competition a§ects all domestic Örms equally.
5:190:15845:12:12:86:539:183:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:A:In this section, we Örst develop the basic structure of our model, and then analyze equilibrium in two
5:191:15947:12:12:72:540:171:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:B:cases.  The Örst case considers trade between two identical countries, and the second introduces di§erences
5:192:16054:12:26:72:281:159:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:B:in aggregate factor endowment across countries.

7:283:19501:12:12:71:539:554:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:B:intensity of goodsíproduction. This is a natural speciÖcation, since preferences and production techniques are
7:284:19611:12:12:71:539:542:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:B:logically separate concepts, and there is no particular empirical reason to think that they are linked. However,
7:285:19723:12:12:71:540:530:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f4:B:this assumption is in sharp contrast to the Heckscher-Ohlin tradition in trade theory. In the canonical2-2-2
7:286:19831:12:11:72:540:518:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:B:model, the two homogeneous goods di§er in their factor intensity, there is a Önite elasticity of substitution
7:287:19940:11:12:72:540:507:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:B:between the goods, and an inÖnite elasticity of substitution across "varieties" within goods.   In their
7:288:20044:12:12:72:539:495:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:B:integration of monopolistic competition into the2-2-2model, Helpman and Krugman (1985) maintain
7:289:20139:12:12:72:540:483:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:B:this ranking of elasticities of substitution in less extreme form: there is a Önite elasticity of substitution
7:290:20249:12:12:72:540:471:10:g_d0_f6:10:g_d0_f2:B:- >1across varieties produced with a given factor intensity, and a smaller elasticity of substitution across
7:291:20357:12:12:72:540:459:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:B:varieties produced with di§erent factor intensities.   The same assumptions on preferences are made by
7:292:20459:12:12:72:540:447:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:B:Bernard, Redding, and Schott (2007) and Burstein and Vogel (2010). Like our model, the model of Romalis
7:293:20562:12:12:72:540:435:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:B:(2004) features monopolistic competition and Cobb-Douglas production where the factor cost shares vary
7:294:20664:12:12:72:540:423:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:B:continuously.  Following Dornbusch, Fischer, and Samuelson (1980), Romalis identiÖes goods with their
7:295:20765:12:12:72:540:411:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:B:factor intensity, and assumes that the elasticity of substitution across goods is one while the elasticity across
7:296:20878:12:12:72:540:399:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:B:varieties within goods is greater than one.  As will become clear in what follows, our decision to break with
7:297:20987:12:12:72:540:387:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:B:this Heckscher-Ohlin tradition and sever the link between preferences and production technology has major
7:298:21092:12:26:72:365:375:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:B:implications for how factor markets respond to trade liberalization.
7:299:21160:26:18:200:410:349:10:g_d0_f7:10:g_d0_f7:?:2.3.  Equilibrium with identical countries

11:599:30397:18:12:86:539:322:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:A:In our model, as in Melitz (2003), exporters are low cost Örms.  In the data, a common Önding is that
11:600:30498:12:12:71:539:310:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:B:exporters are more skill intensive than non-exporting Örms, even within industries. We will present data
11:601:30602:12:12:71:539:298:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:B:below that illustrates the skill bias of exporters for Chile, and the same is true for the United States (see
11:602:30711:12:12:71:539:286:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:B:for example Table 3 in Bernard, Jensen, Redding, and Schott (2007)). In this section we show the factor
11:603:30814:12:12:71:539:274:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:B:market consequences of trade liberalization in the empirically relevant case of skill-biased technology. We
11:604:30921:12:26:71:468:262:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:B:also analyze the case where there is no relationship between technology and skill intensity.,
11:605:31014:26:12:86:539:236:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f2:A:Skill biased technology.If the skill premium is positive(s=w >1);then skill intensive Örms will have
11:606:31114:12:12:71:539:224:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:B:higher costs, controlling for productivity'. Therefore, in our model the only way for exporters to be more
11:607:31220:12:12:71:539:212:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:B:skill-intensive than the average is if skill intensity-and productivity'are positively correlated when Örms
11:608:31327:12:12:71:539:200:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:B:draw their technology parameters. In such a case, high skill intensity is on average associated with high
11:609:31432:12:12:71:540:188:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:B:competitiveness-.  For now we simply assume such a correlation in theex antetechnology distribution
11:610:31531:12:12:71:413:176:10:g_d0_f6:10:g_d0_f2:B:G(-;'), and we will calibrate the correlation in the numerical analysis below.
11:611:31609:12:12:86:539:164:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:A:What does our model imply about the labor market e§ects of opening to trade? Holding factor prices
11:612:31707:12:12:71:539:152:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:B:Öxed for the moment, our model works exactly like Melitz (2003): opening to trade reduces revenue in the
11:613:31811:12:12:71:539:140:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:B:domestic market because of import competition, and creates opportunities for extra revenue in the export
11:614:31915:12:12:71:540:128:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:B:market. In the new equilibrium, the survival cuto§--rises, and with costly trade the export cuto§--xis
11:615:32017:12:12:71:540:116:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:B:higher than--. For new exporters, labor demand rises, while for non-exporters labor demand falls. By our
11:616:32121:12:12:71:539:104:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:B:assumption onG(-;');the exporting Örms are more skill intensive on average than the non-exporting Örms,
11:617:32224:12:15:71:539:92:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:B:so the expansion of the former and the contraction of the latter means a shift up in the relative demand for
11:618:32332:15:0:83:245:77:6:g_d0_f2:8:g_d0_f2:A:9Here we use!(s;w) =sH+wLH+Lto simplify.
12:619:32373:0:12:72:540:710:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:B:skill, equation (36).  To satisfy the relative labor market clearing condition (41), the skill premium must
12:620:32480:12:20:72:156:698:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:B:rise.  We thus have
12:621:32499:20:12:86:539:678:10:g_d0_f22:10:g_d0_f3:A:Proposition 1.Opening to trade between identical countries with skill-biased heterogeneous Örms leads
12:622:32600:12:20:71:297:666:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:C:to a increase in the skill premium in both countries.
12:623:32653:20:24:86:187:646:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:A:Proof: See Appendix 1.
12:624:32675:24:12:71:431:622:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:B:The e§ects on the sets of surviving and exporting Örms are illustrated in Figure 2.
12:625:32758:12:12:86:539:610:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:A:The result that trade liberalization may raise the skill premium appears in other models, as noted in
12:626:32859:12:12:71:539:598:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:B:our introduction.  What is new in our model is the integration of relative labor demand e§ects with Örm
12:627:32962:12:12:71:538:586:10:g_d0_f2:7:g_d0_f2:B:heterogeneity, as well as the ability of the model to match key moments in the data (see Section 3 below).10
12:628:33070:12:12:71:539:574:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:B:Our model predicts that exporters are both more skill-intensive and more productive than non-exporters,
12:629:33173:12:12:71:477:562:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:B:and it is this interaction that generates the increased skill premium with trade liberalization.
12:630:33269:12:12:86:539:550:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:A:There are aggregate welfare gains from opening to trade in our model, but the factor price e§ects leave
12:631:33372:12:12:71:539:538:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:B:open the possibility that unskilled workers may see real wage losses from opening to trade. We investigate
12:632:33478:12:26:71:254:526:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:B:this issue in our numerical analysis below.
12:633:33521:26:12:86:539:500:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f2:A:No skill bias in technology.We now consider the case where the skill share-and productivity'are
12:634:33616:12:12:71:540:488:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:B:independent, so that the theex antetechnology distribution can be written as the product of the marginal
12:635:33720:12:12:71:539:476:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:B:distributions,G(-;') =G-(-)G'('). There are no analytical results for this case in general. However,
12:636:33820:12:11:71:540:464:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:B:if the distribution of'is Pareto and the distribution of-is uniform, we show in Appendix 1 that trade
12:637:33921:11:12:71:539:453:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:B:liberalization increases the survival cuto§ for competitiveness--and reduces the export cuto§--xbut has
12:638:34024:12:12:71:539:441:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:B:has no e§ect on the skill premium. The intuition for this result is straightforward: opening to trade has the
12:639:34133:12:12:71:539:429:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:B:standard pro-competitive e§ects, but the resulting changes in Örm-level relative labor demand are not biased
12:640:34241:12:12:71:539:417:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:B:in favor of either skilled or unskilled labor. We also show that relative factor prices depend only on relative
12:641:34352:12:20:71:274:405:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:B:factor endowments. We collect these results in
12:642:34398:20:12:86:540:385:10:g_d0_f22:10:g_d0_f3:A:Proposition 2.When skill intensity-and productivity'are independent, with'distributed Pareto
12:643:34490:12:12:71:539:373:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:F:and-distributed uniform, and countries are identical, relative factor prices depend only on relative factor
12:644:34597:12:12:71:540:361:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:F:endowments. Trade liberalization raises the survival cuto§--and reduces the export cuto§--xand has no
12:645:34698:12:20:71:190:349:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:C:e§ect on the skill premium.
12:646:34725:20:24:86:187:329:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:A:Proof: See Appendix 1.
12:647:34747:24:12:86:540:305:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:A:Though Proposition 2 only holds for particular choices for the distributions of-and productivity', in
12:648:34848:12:12:71:539:293:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:B:our numerical analysis below we show that trade liberalization between identical countries with no skill bias
12:649:34957:12:12:71:539:281:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:B:in technology has very close to zero e§ect on the skill premium. Propositions 1 and 2 together illustrate
12:650:35062:12:12:71:539:269:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:B:the point that it is skill-bias in technology which leads to factor market e§ects of trade liberalization in our
12:651:35174:12:26:71:100:257:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:B:model.

15:813:41530:0:19:72:288:710:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:C:2.4.5.  Trade liberalization and the skill premium
15:814:41580:19:12:86:539:691:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:A:What e§ects do trade liberalization have in the asymmetric country version of our model?  Full analysis
15:815:41683:12:12:72:540:679:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:B:can only be done numerically, but some insight can be gained through analytical reasoning. In all of what
15:816:41788:12:12:72:489:667:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:B:follows, we assume that countryA("North") is more skill abundant than countryB("South").
15:817:41876:12:12:86:539:655:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:A:Consider the two countries in autarky.  If skilled labor is su¢ ciently scarce, the skill premium will be
15:818:41981:12:15:72:261:643:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:B:positive in both countries, and higher inB,
15:819:42024:15:18:258:272:628:10:g_d0_f11:10:g_d0_f6:A:-s
15:820:42026:18:0:265:272:610:10:g_d0_f6:10:g_d0_f6:A:w
15:821:42027:0:11:274:286:628:10:g_d0_f11:7:g_d0_f8:A:-B
15:822:42029:11:0:290:298:617:10:g_d0_f6:10:g_d0_f6:C:>
15:823:42030:0:18:300:314:628:10:g_d0_f11:10:g_d0_f6:?:-s
15:824:42032:18:0:307:314:610:10:g_d0_f6:10:g_d0_f6:A:w
15:825:42033:0:11:316:328:628:10:g_d0_f11:7:g_d0_f8:A:-A
15:826:42035:11:21:331:540:617:10:g_d0_f6:10:g_d0_f2:A:>1:(59)
15:827:42042:21:12:72:539:596:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:B:We consider two cases. The Örst is the "no skill bias" case, where'and-are uncorrelated. The second,
15:828:42142:12:12:72:540:584:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:B:and empirically relevant, "skill biased" case is where'and-are strongly positively correlated. Skill bias
15:829:42247:12:26:72:364:572:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:B:implies that unit costs and skill intensity are negatively correlated.
15:830:42317:26:12:86:539:546:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f2:A:No skill bias in technology.In the no bias case when (59) holds, in autarky there is anegativeaverage
15:831:42418:12:12:72:540:534:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:B:relationship between unit costs and factor intensity, with more skill intensive Örms having higher unit costs.
15:832:42528:12:12:72:540:522:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:B:In short, having a high skill share is bad news for a Örm: it means that they have higher labor costs without,
15:833:42638:12:12:72:297:510:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:B:on average, any associated technological advantage.
15:834:42689:12:12:86:539:498:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:A:Now consider an opening to costly trade. Holding factor costs Öxed, this will lead to an expansion of the
15:835:42794:12:12:72:540:486:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:B:lower-cost Örms in both countries, and contraction or exit for higher cost Örms.  Because the low-cost Örms
15:836:42901:12:12:72:540:474:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:B:are less skill intensive, this will lead to an increased relative demand for unskilled workers in both countries.
15:837:43014:12:24:72:120:462:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:B:So we have
15:838:43024:24:11:86:539:438:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f2:A:Conjecture 1If'and-are uncorrelated, and autarky skill premiums satisfy (59), then opening to
15:839:43117:11:24:72:358:427:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:B:costly trade leads to a fall in the skill premium in both countries.
15:840:43185:24:12:86:539:403:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:A:We are emphatically no longer in a Heckscher-Ohlin world. The reason is simple: in our model there is
15:841:43286:12:12:72:540:391:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:B:no connection between factor intensity and preferences. As a result, an increase in import competition in our
15:842:43395:12:12:72:540:379:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:B:model, whatever the skill content of the imported goods, a§ects demand for all domestically produced goods
15:843:43501:12:12:72:540:367:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:B:symmetrically. In models with a Heckscher-Ohlin structure, by contrast, an increase in import competition
15:844:43606:12:12:72:540:355:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:B:changes the relative demand for domestically produced goods. Because relative goods demand is directly
15:845:43708:12:12:72:540:343:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:B:linked to relative factor demands, Stolper-Samuelson type results follow. In our model the factor price e§ects
15:846:43818:12:12:72:540:331:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:B:of opening to trade have nothing to do with demand and everything to do with supply: since skill-intensive
15:847:43924:12:26:72:461:319:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:B:Örms have higher costs, opening to trade reduces the relative demand for skilled workers.
15:848:44013:26:12:86:539:293:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f2:A:Skill biased technology.We now turn to the empirically relevant case, where skill intensity is associated
15:849:44118:12:12:72:540:281:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:B:with higher factor costs but also better technology on average. We focus on the case where the technology
15:850:44223:12:12:72:540:269:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:B:e§ect is dominant, so that on average more skill intensive Örms have lower unit costs. Now consider an
15:851:44325:12:12:72:540:257:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:B:opening to costly trade.  Holding factor costs Öxed, this will lead to an expansion of the lower-cost Örms in
15:852:44434:12:12:72:540:245:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:B:both countries, and contraction or exit for higher cost Örms.  This will lead to an increase in the relative
15:853:44542:12:12:72:540:233:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:B:demand for skill in both countries.  To restore factor market equilibrium, the skill premium must rise in
15:854:44647:12:24:72:282:221:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:B:both countries. We summarize this reasoning as
15:855:44693:24:12:86:540:197:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f2:A:Conjecture 2If productivity'and-are strongly positively correlated, then opening to costly trade
15:856:44789:12:23:72:304:185:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:B:leads to a rise in the skill premium in both countries.
15:857:44844:23:24:86:450:162:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:A:We demonstrate below that Conjectures 1 and 2 hold in our numerical simulations.
15:858:44924:24:12:86:539:138:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:A:The inisght that opening to trade raises the skill premium globally is similar to what we showed for
15:859:45024:12:0:72:392:126:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:B:identical countries in Proposition 1, and the mechanism is the same here.
16:860:45099:0:12:86:539:710:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f2:A:Trade patterns.Although the factor price e§ects of opening to trade in our model are very di§erent
16:861:45197:12:12:71:539:698:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:B:from what is found in Heckscher-Ohlin models, the trade patterns are broadly in line with Heckscher-Ohlin
16:862:45302:12:12:71:539:686:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:B:predictions, although the mechanism is di§erent.  Because the skill premium remains lower inAthan in
16:863:45402:12:12:72:540:674:10:g_d0_f6:10:g_d0_f2:B:Bafter liberalization,Awill have a comparative advantage in high skilled goods, and production in each
16:864:45504:12:12:72:540:662:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:B:country will shift toward comparative advantage goods.   In our model, what we mean by comparative
16:865:45602:12:12:72:539:650:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:B:advantage is that high-skill exporters are more likely to come fromA, while low-skill exporters are more
16:866:45706:12:12:72:540:638:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:B:likely to come fromB. The specialization pattern is illustrated in Figure 3, which is drawn on the assumption
16:867:45815:12:12:71:540:626:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:B:that the overall level of competition is less intense inBthan inA(this is not essential, but it is what we
16:868:45921:12:26:71:235:614:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:B:Önd in our numerical analysis below).
16:869:45958:26:18:131:479:588:10:g_d0_f7:10:g_d0_f7:?:2.5.  Quality competition: an alternative interpretation of the model
16:870:46027:18:12:86:539:570:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:A:Our model assumes two-dimensional heterogeneity in Örmsí technology, combined with symmetry in
16:871:46121:12:12:71:539:558:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:B:demand. Firmsírevenue and proÖts are indexed by their inverse unit cost-;and larger Örms charge lower
16:872:46222:12:12:71:539:546:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:B:prices because they have lower unit costs (see equation 10). A way to summarize this is that in our model (as
16:873:46331:12:12:71:539:534:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:B:well as in the models of Melitz (2003), Bernard, Redding, and Schott (2007), and others) Örms "compete on
16:874:46436:12:12:71:539:522:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:B:cost." This conáicts with evidence amassed by many authors, including Verhoogen (2008) and Baldwin and
16:875:46538:12:12:71:539:510:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:B:Harrigan (2011), that exporters more often "compete on quality", with more successful Örms actually having
16:876:46644:12:12:71:539:498:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:B:higher costs and prices than less successful Örms. In this subsection we show that our model can easily be
16:877:46750:12:12:71:538:486:10:g_d0_f2:7:g_d0_f2:B:converted into a model of quality competition, with quality being positively correlated with skill intensity.11
16:878:46861:12:12:71:539:474:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:B:For readers uninterested in the details, the punchline is simple: none of the implications of our model for
16:879:46968:12:12:71:516:462:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:B:trade, gains from trade, or factor prices are a§ected by rewriting it as a model of quality competition.
16:880:47072:12:31:86:516:450:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:A:Suppose that'is not a parameter of cost but of demand. In particular, let the utility function be

17:945:49127:12:12:72:449:567:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:B:quality-competition model in exactly the way we proceeded above for the main model.

17:947:49319:12:12:72:540:543:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:B:and quality fromG(-;'). As argued by Kugler and Verhoogen (2010), producing high quality is likely to
17:948:49420:12:12:72:540:531:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:B:require a more skilled labor force, which implies thatCov(-;')>0;just as in the main model. With this
17:949:49521:12:12:72:523:519:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:B:assumption, all our results about the labor market e§ects of trade liberalization go through unchanged.
17:950:49624:12:12:86:539:507:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:A:Our conclusion from this subsection is simple: the quality competition variant of our model of skill biased
17:951:49731:12:12:72:540:495:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:B:heterogeneous Örms has the same workings and implications as the main model. With this point established,
17:952:49836:12:27:72:405:483:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:B:we put aside the quality competition variant for the remainder of the paper.
17:953:49912:27:22:195:416:456:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:C:3.  EMPIRICAL AND NUMERICAL ANALYSIS
17:954:49948:22:12:86:539:434:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:A:In this section we bring numbers to bear on our model in three ways. First, we examine a plant-level data
17:955:50053:12:12:72:540:422:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:B:set from a small open economy, Chile 1990-1995, and show that key features of the Chilean data match the
17:956:50157:12:12:72:540:410:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:B:key assumptions of our model. Second, we use the Chilean data in 1995 to calibrate our identical country
17:957:50261:12:12:72:540:398:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:B:model. Lastly, we use the numerical version of our model to illustrate its workings, with a focus on the
17:958:50365:12:26:72:293:386:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:B:e§ects of trade liberalization on the skill premium.
17:959:50417:26:18:231:380:360:10:g_d0_f7:10:g_d0_f7:?:3.1.  Chilean plant level data
17:960:50447:18:12:86:539:342:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:A:Our data source is the Annual National Industrial Survey of Chile (Encuesta Nacional Industrial Annual,
17:961:50550:12:12:72:540:330:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:B:or ENIA).12The ENIA is conducted annually by the Chilean government statistical o¢ ce (Instituto Nacional
17:962:50655:12:12:72:540:318:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:B:de Estadistica, or INE). The ENIA covers the universe of Chilean manufacturing plants with 10 or more
17:963:50756:12:12:72:540:306:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:B:workers. Pavcnik (2002) indicates that in 1979-1986 more than 90% of Chilean manufacturing Örms had
17:964:50855:12:12:72:540:294:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:B:only one plant, so the distinction between plants and Örms is unlikely to be very important. Our concept of
17:965:50962:12:12:72:540:282:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:B:a skilled worker is captured in the ENIA by white collar workers.13For each of these plants we construct
17:966:51066:12:22:72:172:270:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:B:the following variables:
17:967:51090:22:20:86:145:248:10:g_d0_f5:10:g_d0_f2:A:-log revenue
17:968:51102:20:19:86:312:228:10:g_d0_f5:10:g_d0_f2:A:-export intensity = export revenue / total revenue
17:969:51152:19:20:86:169:209:10:g_d0_f5:10:g_d0_f2:A:-log average wage
17:970:51169:20:20:86:229:189:10:g_d0_f5:10:g_d0_f2:A:-white collar employment share
17:971:51199:20:20:86:215:169:10:g_d0_f5:10:g_d0_f2:A:-white collar wage bill share
17:972:51228:20:16:86:270:149:10:g_d0_f5:10:g_d0_f2:A:-export status (1 if export revenue>0).
17:973:51267:16:9:79:315:133:6:g_d0_f2:8:g_d0_f2:?:12We thank James Tybout for generosly providing us this data.
17:974:51328:9:12:79:540:124:6:g_d0_f2:8:g_d0_f2:F:13Proxying skill by "white collar" is problematic, though it is (by necessity) common practice in studies that use plant
17:975:51448:12:10:72:540:112:8:g_d0_f2:8:g_d0_f2:F:level data.  Berman, Bound, and Griliches (1994) show that for the United States, the production/non-production worker
17:976:51566:10:0:72:303:102:8:g_d0_f2:8:g_d0_f2:F:classiÖcation is a good proxy for skilled and unskilled workers.
18:977:51632:0:12:86:539:710:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:A:Standard regression-based methods (such as Olley-Pakes) of computing plant-level total factor productiv-
18:978:51736:12:12:72:540:698:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:B:ity (TFP) are not applicable here. The reason is simple: regression-based TFP calculations need to assume
18:979:51841:12:12:72:540:686:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:B:that factor shares are constant across all plants. This is both empirically false (as we show immediately
18:980:51946:12:12:72:540:674:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:B:below) and contradicts the mechanism of our theory. The absence of plant-level TFP is not a drawback for
18:981:52050:12:12:72:540:662:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:B:our purposes, as our minimum distance estimator (discussed below) identiÖes all the necesary parameters
18:982:52153:12:26:72:253:650:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:B:using the data moments that we do have.
18:983:52192:26:19:72:267:624:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:C:3.1.1.  The 1995 Chilean plant cross section
18:984:52236:19:11:86:539:605:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:A:5,112 plants were surveyed in 1995. We eliminated 163 plants that did not report positive revenues. We
18:985:52338:11:12:72:540:594:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:B:also eliminated 346 plants that had either white collar employment share or white collar wage bill share
18:986:52442:12:12:72:540:582:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:B:equal to 0 or 1 (these coincide 93% of the times). These plants account for 5.3% of total revenue in the
18:987:52546:12:12:72:540:570:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:B:sample and are 30% smaller on average, but their distribution of revenues is not very dissimilar from the
18:988:52651:12:12:72:540:558:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:B:rest, so that this elimination does not a§ect much the overall distribution of revenues. However, only 4.6%
18:989:52758:12:12:72:343:546:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:B:of these plants export, compared to 24.3% of the other plants.
18:990:52820:12:12:86:539:534:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:A:Thus, we use a cross section of 4,603 plants in 1995, of which 24.3% are exporters. Table 1 shows that
18:991:52922:12:12:72:540:522:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:B:exporting plants are larger and more skill-intensive than non-exporters. The distribution of log revenues for
18:992:53031:12:12:72:540:510:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:B:exporters is less skewed to the right, i.e. the largest exporters are closer to their respective mean than the
18:993:53141:12:12:72:540:498:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:B:largest Örms that do not export. As with log revenues, we see that the distribution of the skill employment
18:994:53248:12:12:72:540:486:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:B:and wage bill shares for exporters are less skewed to the right.  Figure 4 shows the wage bill shares for
18:995:53353:12:12:72:540:474:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:B:white collar workers among exporters and non-exporters. The most interesting aspect of Figure 4 is not the
18:996:53459:12:12:72:540:462:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:B:di§erence in median skill shares (which is well known) but the variability: many exporters have low skill
18:997:53564:12:12:72:540:450:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:B:shares, and conversely for non-exporters. In fact, the variation of the skill share within the exporter/non-
18:998:53672:12:12:72:540:438:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:B:exporter categories is essentially the same as the overall variation.14Table 2 shows that the correlation
18:999:53777:12:12:72:540:426:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:B:between log revenues and the white collar wage bill share is positive, and slightly more so for exporters.
18:1000:53883:12:12:72:540:414:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:B:Figure 5 illustrates this relationship, and the variability in this Ögure motivates the key feature of our model:
18:1001:53996:12:12:72:540:402:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:B:the positive but imperfect correlation between skill intensity and size. We exploit these di§erences when
18:1002:54101:12:12:72:528:390:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:B:choosing parameter values for the joint distribution of skilled labor share and productivity in the model.
18:1003:54207:12:12:86:539:378:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:A:In addition, the Örm-level skill share varies both within industries and across industries, but the within
18:1004:54313:12:12:72:540:366:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:B:variation is much larger: between 4-digit ISIC industries, the standard deviation of the skill share is 0.11,
18:1005:54422:12:25:72:445:354:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:B:while within industries the standard deviation is more than half again as big, at 0.17.
18:1006:54509:25:19:72:259:329:10:g_d0_f3:10:g_d0_f3:C:3.1.2.  The 1990-1995 Chilean plant panel
18:1007:54550:19:12:86:539:310:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:A:In principle there are at least two mechanisms that can account for a correlation between export status
18:1008:54653:12:12:72:540:298:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:B:and skill intensity: skill-intensive plants select into exporting, or exporters choose to become more skill
18:1009:54760:12:12:72:540:286:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:B:intensive. These mechanisms are not mutually exclusive. In our model we focus on the Örst mechanism,
18:1010:54860:12:12:72:540:274:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:B:though as noted in our introduction there is evidence in some data sets for the latter mechanism as well. In
18:1011:54968:12:12:72:382:262:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:B:this subsection we brieáy investigate this question in our Chilean data.
18:1012:55040:12:12:86:539:250:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:A:Our tool is a series of very simple panel regressions with plant and year Öxed e§ects, along with indicators
18:1013:55148:12:23:72:144:238:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:B:of export status:
18:1014:55165:23:18:246:366:215:10:g_d0_f6:10:g_d0_f6:A:yit=-i+-t+\f0xit+"it;
18:1015:55186:18:12:72:539:197:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:B:whereyitis an outcome of interest (log revenue or wage bill share of skilled workers),xitis a vector of
18:1016:55289:12:12:72:540:185:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:B:export participation indicators, and"itis interpreted simply as the prediction error from a linear projection.
18:1017:55399:12:12:72:344:173:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:B:Estimation is by OLS with standard errors clustered by plant.
18:1018:55460:12:12:86:539:161:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:A:Table 3 shows our results.  The Panel A tells a simple and utterly unsurprising story: entering into
18:1019:55560:12:12:72:540:149:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:B:exporting leads to big increases in revenue. Panel B, which investigates within-plant variation in the skill
18:1020:55668:12:12:72:540:137:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:B:share over time, shows no statistically signiÖcant evidence of skill upgrading when plants enter into exporting.
18:1021:55780:12:12:72:540:125:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:B:We get virtually the same results when the regressand is the skilled employment share. As noted in the
18:1022:55882:12:12:72:540:113:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:B:introduction, this result is consistent with the evidence for the U.S. (Bernard and Jensen (1997), Bernard
18:1023:55988:12:15:72:540:101:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:B:and Jensen (1999)) and Canada (Treáer (2004)). Our conclusion from this simple exercise is that the Chilean
18:1024:56095:15:13:79:539:86:6:g_d0_f2:8:g_d0_f2:F:14The overall standard deviation of the skill share is 0.197. Within exporters the standard deviation is 0.205, while within
18:1025:56219:13:0:72:484:73:8:g_d0_f2:8:g_d0_f2:F:non-exporters it is 0.185. TheR2of a regression of the skill share on an indicator for export status is just 0.07.
19:1026:56335:0:12:72:540:710:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:B:plant level data set is a reasonable source of information for calibrating some of the key parameters of our
19:1027:56443:12:26:72:214:698:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:B:model, and we turn to this next.
19:1028:56475:26:19:125:486:672:10:g_d0_f7:10:g_d0_f7:?:3.2.  Modelling the correlation between skill intensity and productivity
19:1029:56547:19:12:86:539:653:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:A:A key innovation in our model is that we allow for positive but imperfect correlation between skill intensity
19:1030:56656:12:12:72:540:641:10:g_d0_f6:10:g_d0_f2:B:-and productivity':This is motivated by the cross-sectional evidence that is vividly illustrated in Figures
19:1031:56763:12:12:72:540:629:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:B:4 and 5. To implement this we Örst specify the marginal distributions of-and', and then model the
19:1032:56860:12:12:71:184:617:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:B:correlation between them.

21:1144:63538:12:12:86:539:522:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:A:The purely Melitz side of the model is illustrated in the four panels of Figure 6a.  Trade liberalization
21:1145:63643:12:12:72:540:510:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:B:leads to heightened competition, which is manifested in progressively higher survival cost cuto§s and lower
21:1146:63750:12:12:72:540:498:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:B:export cost cuto§s.  The result is less equilibrium entry, and the mass of Örms is smaller.  Unlike in Melitz
21:1147:63859:12:12:72:540:486:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:B:(2003), the weighted average productivity of active Örms is not a useful summary statistic, since it is unit
21:1148:63967:12:12:72:540:474:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:B:cost, rather than productivityper se, that determines Örm success in our model. Instead, we focus on real
21:1149:64072:12:12:72:540:462:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:B:GDP (nominal GDP divided by the aggregate price index) as a summary of the economy-wide e§ects of
21:1150:64169:12:12:72:540:450:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:B:trade liberalization. As expected, real GDP rises substantially as trade barriers are reduced, with the move
21:1151:64277:12:12:72:346:438:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:B:from autarky to costless trade raising real GDP by 19 percent.
21:1152:64339:12:12:86:539:426:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:A:The novelty in our model comes from the factor market consequences of trade liberalization, which are
21:1153:64440:12:12:72:540:414:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:B:illustrated in the three panels of Figure 6b. The skill premium rises substantially as trade barriers fall, from
21:1154:64552:12:12:72:539:402:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:B:2.84 in autarky to 2.98 for moderate trade costs (-= 1.4), an increase of 5 percent. Complete elimination
21:1155:64657:12:12:72:540:390:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:B:of variable trade costs raises the skill premium by 7.7 percent compared to autarky.  Real skilled wages
21:1156:64761:12:12:72:540:378:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:B:rise by almost ten percent in the move from autarky to moderate trade costs, while unskilled workers see
21:1157:64865:12:12:72:540:366:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:B:more modest real wage increases of 4 percent. It is notable that all workers share in the gains from trade
21:1158:64971:12:12:72:540:354:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:B:despite the rise in relative skill demand.  The reason for this is the improvement in aggregate e¢ ciency
21:1159:65076:12:11:72:540:342:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:B:caused by trade liberalization, with high-cost low-skill Örms exiting or contracting and low-cost, high-skill
21:1160:65185:11:12:72:224:331:10:g_d0_f2:7:g_d0_f2:B:Örms entering the export market.19
21:1161:65219:12:12:86:539:319:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:A:The second column of Panel A of Table 4 summarizes how our results di§er when we nearly double skill
21:1162:65319:12:12:72:540:307:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:B:abundance, from 0.26 (the level in our Chilean data set) to 0.5. The gains from trade are slightly higher, and
21:1163:65429:12:12:72:540:295:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:B:the increase in the skill premium is much smaller, rising just 3.5 percent in the move from autarky to costless
21:1164:65540:12:12:72:540:283:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:B:trade. The increase in real wages is 22 percent for skilled workers and 18 percent for unskilled workers. The
21:1165:65649:12:12:72:540:271:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:B:explanation for this contrast is that skill bias puts a premium on skilled workers when trade is liberalized:
21:1166:65758:12:12:72:540:259:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:B:when they are much more abundant, the economy as a whole gains more, with the gains from trade more
21:1167:65857:12:26:72:301:247:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:B:evenly shared between skilled and unskilled workers.

21:1176:66667:15:12:79:540:116:6:g_d0_f2:8:g_d0_f2:F:18We maintain Öxed costs for exporting that are equal to the Öxed costs of entering the home market throughout. For brevity,
21:1177:66791:12:7:72:315:104:8:g_d0_f2:8:g_d0_f2:F:we say that trade is "costless" when variable trade costs are zero.

22:1181:67160:0:12:72:540:710:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:B:Önal three columns of Panel A of Table 4. In computing the equilibria, we choose global nominal GDP as
22:1182:67262:12:12:72:371:698:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:B:our numeraire, so nominal values are in units of a common currency.
22:1183:67329:12:12:86:539:686:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:A:In this numerical exercise we are not attempting to fully calibrate the model to any particular pair
22:1184:67429:12:12:72:540:674:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:B:of countries or trade liberalization episode.  By abstracting from di§erences in autarky country size and
22:1185:67534:12:12:72:540:662:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:B:technology distributions, we isolate the mechanisms which are new to our model, which are the interactions
22:1186:67640:12:12:72:540:650:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:B:between trade liberalization, factor proportions di§erences, and skill biased heterogeneous Örms. Thus the
22:1187:67746:12:12:72:540:638:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:B:quantitative results here should be interpreted as plausible numerical examples rather than estimates of the
22:1188:67854:12:12:72:285:626:10:g_d0_f2:7:g_d0_f2:B:e§ects of an actual trade liberalization episode.20
22:1189:67905:12:12:86:539:614:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:A:The four panels of Figure 7a illustrate the expected Melitz-type mechanisms: as trade is liberalized,
22:1190:68006:12:12:71:539:602:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:B:entry becomes more di¢ cult, the mass of Örms falls, and the share of exporters gets monotonically higher.
22:1191:68112:12:12:71:539:590:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:B:Aggregate gains from trade are shown by rising real GDP in both countries. CountryBis a somewhat less
22:1192:68213:12:12:71:539:578:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:B:competitive market thanA, which is seen in the higher cost cuto§s forAand the associated higher probability
22:1193:68320:12:12:71:540:566:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:B:of entry inB. The reason for this is an interaction between skill bias and the higher skill premium inB:
22:1194:68424:12:12:71:539:554:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:B:skill bias implies that the lowest cost Örms are the most skill-intensive on average in both countries, but
22:1195:68531:12:12:71:540:542:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:B:because of the higher skill premium inBthe cost advantage for the best Örms is smaller inBthan inA.
22:1196:68630:12:12:71:540:530:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:B:This di§erence in theex antecost distributions in the two countries is illustrated in Figure 8:Bhas more
22:1197:68734:12:12:71:539:518:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:B:low-end and fewer high-end Örms than doesA;in both autarky and costless trade equilibria. Thus high cost
22:1198:68838:12:12:71:539:506:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:B:Örms inBearn more revenue than high cost Örms inA, which makes survival easier. A áip side to this is
22:1199:68939:12:12:71:539:494:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:B:thatAgains a bit more from trade: trade puts a premium on skill, andAís greater skill abundance and
22:1200:69038:12:12:71:518:482:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:B:lower skill premium means it can better take advantage of trade liberalization than can skill-scarceB.
22:1201:69140:12:12:86:539:470:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:A:The four panels of Figure 7b illustrate Conjecture 2: trade liberalization raises the skill premium in both
22:1202:69247:12:11:71:539:458:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:B:countries, and in our numerical exercise this e§ect continues all the way to costless trade. As can be seen in
22:1203:69357:11:12:71:539:447:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:B:Table 4, the increases in the skill premium are fairly modest: in the move from autarky to moderate trade
22:1204:69462:12:12:71:539:435:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:B:costs of-= 1:4, the skill premium increases by 2.1 percent inAand 5.7 percent inB, with increases of 3.5 and
22:1205:69570:12:12:71:539:423:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:B:8.5 percent respectively when moving all the way to costless trade. An implication is that our example world
22:1206:69678:12:12:71:539:411:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:B:economy features relative factor price divergence as trade is liberalized, with the skill premium inBrelative
22:1207:69787:12:12:71:539:399:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:B:toAincreasing from 2.30 to 2.41. This result is very much at odds with the factor price equalization forces
22:1208:69894:12:12:71:532:387:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:B:in Heckscher-Ohlin models. This factor price divergence result need not hold in all numerical simulations.
22:1209:70000:12:12:86:539:375:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:A:While the factor price implications of our model are completely at odds with Heckscher-Ohlin models,
22:1210:70100:12:12:71:539:363:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:B:our model does feature trade in factor services that is predicted by factor abundance. This is illustrated in
22:1211:70209:12:12:71:539:351:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:B:Figure 9, which shows that the net factor content of trade increases quite rapidly with falls in variable trade
22:1212:70320:12:12:71:102:339:10:g_d0_f2:7:g_d0_f2:B:costs.21
22:1213:70328:12:12:86:539:327:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:A:To illustrate the key importance of skill bias in generating our results, in panel B of Table 4 we report
22:1214:70433:12:12:71:540:315:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:B:results when there is zeroex antecorrelation between skill intensity and productivity. To compute these
22:1215:70536:12:12:71:540:303:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:B:results, the only change we make is to the copula parameter-:Di§erences in factor abundance still generate
22:1216:70642:12:12:71:539:291:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:B:di§erences in the skill premium, but the level of the skill premium is much lower than in panel A. Trade
22:1217:70746:12:12:71:539:279:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:B:liberalization leads to gains from trade which are comparable to the skill-bias case, but relative factor prices
22:1218:70858:12:12:71:539:267:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:B:are virtually unchanged despite large net trade in factor services (similar to what is seen in Figure 9 but not
22:1219:70969:12:12:71:539:255:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:B:shown in the interest of space). The skill premium does fall very slightly in each case as trade is liberalized,
22:1220:71081:12:12:71:539:243:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:B:so our numerical results are consistent with Conjecture 1. This illustrates again the absence of Stolper-
22:1221:71186:12:12:71:539:231:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:B:Samuelson forces in our model: when factor intensities are unrelated to preferences, trade liberalization does
22:1222:71296:12:12:71:298:219:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:B:not raise the skill premium unless there is skill-bias.
22:1223:71351:12:12:86:539:207:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:A:As a Önal exercise, we consider convergence in relative factor endowments. We consider our base case
22:1224:71451:12:13:71:540:195:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:B:of skill biased technology(-= 11)and moderate trade costs(-= 1:4), and both countries have the same
22:1225:71550:13:12:71:540:182:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:B:population. We begin with(H=L)B= 0:1;and increase human capital inBuntil it reaches the level inA.
22:1226:71648:12:12:71:540:170:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:B:Table 5 reports the results. Interestingly,Ais essentially indi§erent to human capital accumulation inB,
22:1227:71752:12:12:71:539:158:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:B:with tiny increases in real wages and the skill premium. At Örst glance the tiny e§ect that human capital
22:1228:71857:12:12:71:539:146:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:B:accumulation inBhas on factor markets inAmight seem puzzling, since net trade in factor services are
22:1229:71957:12:14:71:539:134:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:B:changing quite dramatically. The reason is the absence of Stolper-Samuelson e§ects in our model: though the
22:1230:72064:14:13:79:539:120:6:g_d0_f2:8:g_d0_f2:F:20The quantitative exercises in Burstein and Vogel (2010) and Bernard, Redding, and Schott (2007), for example, should in
22:1231:72185:13:9:72:86:107:8:g_d0_f2:6:g_d0_f2:F:our view be interpreted in the same way.21
22:1232:72227:9:10:87:540:98:8:g_d0_f2:8:g_d0_f2:F:We calculate the factor content of trade as follows. For exports, we use the exporting countryís unit factor requirements.
22:1233:72349:10:9:72:539:88:8:g_d0_f2:8:g_d0_f2:F:For imports, we use the exporting countryís factor requirements, deáated by the iceberg transport cost factor-:This explains
22:1234:72473:9:0:72:508:79:8:g_d0_f2:8:g_d0_f2:F:why the two panels of Figure 9 are not quite mirror images. When-= 1;the net factor contents sum to zero exactly.
23:1235:72588:0:12:72:540:710:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:B:average good imported byAfromBis becoming more skill intensive asBdevelops, this has no direct e§ect
23:1236:72688:12:12:72:540:698:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:B:on the relative demand for more and less skill-intensive goods produced inA. In contrast,Bexperiences a
23:1237:72791:12:12:72:540:686:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:B:precipitous drop in the skill premium as real GDP rises, with real skilled wages falling and more than all of
23:1238:72900:12:12:72:539:674:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:B:the 28 percent increase in GDP going to unskilled workers. The response inBof the skill premium to factor
23:1239:73005:12:12:72:540:662:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:B:accumulation is consistent with a long-run labor demand elasticity of about -2, which is within the range
23:1240:73110:12:27:72:433:650:10:g_d0_f2:7:g_d0_f2:B:found in the labor economics literature (see for example Autor and Katz (1999)).22
23:1241:73192:27:22:263:348:623:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:A:4.  CONCLUSION
23:1242:73206:22:12:86:539:601:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:A:In this paper we propose a new model that explains why trade liberalization can be associated with a
23:1243:73306:12:12:71:539:589:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:B:rising skill premium in both rich and poor countries. Our model has two dimensions in which Örms di§er,
23:1244:73409:12:12:71:539:577:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:B:skill intensity and productivity, and our assumption that these two attributes are positively correlated is
23:1245:73516:12:12:71:539:565:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:B:veriÖed using Chilean Örm-level data. Because of this correlation, opening to trade shifts up the relative
23:1246:73622:12:12:71:539:553:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:B:demand for skilled workers, as low-cost, skill-intensive Örms expand to seize new export opportunities and
23:1247:73728:12:12:71:539:541:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:B:high-cost, low-skill Örms contract or exit in the face of greater import competition. In equilibrium, the skill
23:1248:73839:12:12:71:539:529:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:B:premium rises when trade is liberalized between identical countries. It is possible that less skilled workers
23:1249:73948:12:12:71:539:517:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:B:will see their real wages fall in such a scenario if their nominal wages fall by more than the reduction in the
23:1250:74059:12:12:71:539:505:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:B:price level, though this does not occur in our simulations: all workers gain from liberalization even as the
23:1251:74167:12:12:71:156:493:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:B:skill premium rises.
23:1252:74187:12:12:86:539:481:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:A:When countries di§er in their relative factor endowments, opening to trade also leads to an increase in
23:1253:74290:12:11:71:539:469:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:B:the skill premium in both countries, and in our example economy there is relative factor price divergence,
23:1254:74396:11:12:71:539:458:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:B:with the skill premium rising by more in the skill-scarce country. This result is at odds with the Stolper-
23:1255:74503:12:12:71:539:446:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:B:Samuelson theorem, which predicts that trade liberalization should lead to a fall in the skill premium in
23:1256:74608:12:12:71:539:434:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:B:the skill-abundant country and the opposite in the skill-scarce country. The reason is that our model makes
23:1257:74715:12:12:71:539:422:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:B:a simple and intuitive departure from one of the key assumptions in the Heckscher-Ohlin tradition: we
23:1258:74816:12:12:71:539:410:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:B:assume that the elasticity of substitution in demand is common across goods, rather than being higher for
23:1259:74921:12:12:71:539:398:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:B:goods with the same factor intensity. Although our model has a Heckscher-Ohlin áavor in its focus on the
23:1260:75025:12:12:71:539:386:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:B:interaction between goods with di§erent factor intensities and countries with di§erent factor endowments,
23:1261:75130:12:12:71:539:374:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:B:this seemingly small change in assumptions means that the model works quite di§erently than models in the
23:1262:75235:12:12:71:186:362:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:B:Heckscher-Ohlin tradition.
23:1263:75261:12:12:86:539:350:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:A:Quantitatively, the factor price e§ects we Önd are small relative both to the size of gains from trade in
23:1264:75366:12:12:71:539:338:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:B:our model and to the changes in the skill premium in the United States and around the world. Thus we see
23:1265:75470:12:12:71:539:326:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:B:our results as consistent with the view that trade liberalization can lead to large welfare gains without being
23:1266:75581:12:240:71:280:314:10:g_d0_f2:10:g_d0_f2:B:a major contributor to growing wage inequality.
